For example, civil rights for minorities, womens rights, gay rights, animal rights, political correctness, and so on : ALL of these have unfolded in my life, and ALL are steps in the increasing general respect for people and life of all types. B000002HMH|4.0|I'm a fan of metal, and a fan of Metallica. But I'm also a fan of MUSIC. It's easy to argue that Metallica lost some of their heavy metal edge with this album - the guitars aren't as thick, and the album is lacking in speed. However, the actual MUSIC on the album is still just fine. With the Load album, Metallica alienated a lot of fans who weren't already alienated by the Black Album (a masterpiece I might add). Metal purists hate this album - and it is understandable. There are songs that would be rather disheartening to fans of the first four Metallica albums, such as the radio-ready hit singles \\"Until It Sleeps\\" (Metallica's first and only top 10 hit and a rock chart topper) and \\"Hero Of The Day\\" (only peaked at number 60, but also topped the rock chart). Even more disheartening to those fans would be \\"Mama Said,\\" a full-phledged ballad with a country twang to it; at first listen that one came as quite the shock to me. Aside from those really, the material on this album is not all that different from the template they created for a sound on the Black Album, so why the fuss?<br /><br />Personally, I say if you find those 3 particular tracks bad then skip 'em and try and enjoy the rest of it for what it is. The opening trio of \\"Ain't My B*tch,\\" \\"2x4\\" and \\"The House Jack Built\\" all fit right in with the Black Album sound. \\"King Nothing,\\" another hit (only peaked at number 90 but also made it to number 3 on the rock chart), basically sounds like \\"Enter Sandman\\" backwards; I might have a problem with this if not for the fact that it ends with a somewhat humorous nod to that. The remainder of the album does sometimes tend to drag a bit; I will admit that songs like \\"Bleeding Me\\" and \\"The Outlaw Torn\\" feel a bit overly long. At the same time, there are some brilliant album tracks buried in between them: the bluesy \\"Poor Twisted Me,\\" and the rockin' \\"Wasting My Hate\\" and \\"Thorn Within.\\" The two more bloated compositions slightly weigh the album down, although if I'm in the right mood they will satisfy me. Also, \\"Cure\\" and \\"Ronnie\\" feel kinda like filler, they are among the less memorable tracks in Metallica's catalog.<br /><br />Honestly, after listening to this several times through I just don't understand the fuss. Arguments can be made against this era of Metallica like, \\"They sold out,\\" \\"They cut their hair,\\" \\"They abandoned their roots,\\" \\"It's not a full-on metal album\\" - but I don't care. Metallica's early material is undeniably the best work they put out, but the fact that they were able to move on from it and make music that appeals to a more commercial audience without completely destroying themselves in the process says a lot. Load, if taken for what it is, is not a GREAT album by any means. But it is a GOOD album, at the very least, and one that should not be shunned as it is by so many people who swear by the first four albums.the right mood they will satisfy me. Also, \\"Cure\\" and \\"Ronnie\\" feel kinda like filler, they are among the less memorable tracks in Metallica's catalog.<br /><br />Honestly, after listening to this several times through I just don't understand the fuss. But it is a GOOD album, at the very least, and one that should not be shunned as it is by so many people who swear by the first four albums.

0307951529|4.0|\\"The $100 Startup: Reinvent The Way You Make A Living, Do What You Love, And Create A New Future\\" by Chris Guillebeau is an interesting and informative book for those who are or want to be entrepreneurs.  It is not about a specific business that you can start for $100, but rather a book about a new model of doing business that the author calls a microbusiness revolution, or a way of earning a good living while crafting a life of independence and purpose.<br /><br />The two themes in the book are freedom and value.  The author states that we are all looking for freedom and value is the way to achieve it.  The book is then filled with case studies of people who have done just that.  They have obtained freedom through creating value for others.  While the author uses personal examples, he also uses many examples from the research he's done on people who have created businesses, with little start up costs, but provided value to others in a way that enabled them to grow the business and provide the lifestyle they desired.<br /><br />Make no mistake, this is not a get rich quick book.  Building a business takes work, and lots of it.  However, the author believes (and so do I) that you can transition to a meaningful life oriented toward something you love to do and make money doing it.  Others have done it, and this book shows the path they took.  The author's premise is that you can also follow this path to create the freedom you crave.<br /><br />The book is divided into three parts, the first, \\"Unexpected Entrepreneurs\\" contains five chapters that deal with Renaissance, Give Them the Fish, Follow You Passion...Maybe, The Rise of the Roaming Entrepreneur, and The New Demographics.  There is some good advice in these chapters, especially in regards to figuring out what you will do, and how not everything will be a great business.  Reality checking is important.<br /><br />Part Two, \\"Taking It To The Streets\\" has five chapters that deal with The One-Page Business Plan, An Offer You Can't Refuse, Launch, Hustling: The Gentle Art of Self-Promotion, and Show Me the Money.  One of the things I really liked in part two was the motivation to act.  Planning is important and good, but you must act.  The author shares his one-page business plan worksheet.<br /><br />The third part, \\"Leverage And Next Steps\\" contains the final four chapters on Moving On Up, How to Franchise Yourself, going Long, and But What If I Fail?  Again, this part has some good information for some people in certain businesses.  My favorite line was at the end of the book when the author says the most important lesson in the whole book is \\"Don't waste your time living someone else's life.<br /><br />The book also points to on-line resources to help further the guidance this book provides.  Overall, I found the book motivating, and containing some valuable information for the would be entrepreneur who wants more freedom.  The world is changing and the way people do business is changing.  This book will help you ride the wave of that change.<br /><br />Reviewed by Alain B. Burrese, J.D, author of the Tough Guy Wisdom series and others.Offer You Can't Refuse, Launch, Hustling: The Gentle Art of Self-Promotion, and Show Me the Money. 1400032717|3.0|I read some professional reviews of this book before deciding to read it, and have read some more after reading (as well as many Amazon customer reviews) and I've had an interesting realization. The central conceit of this book provokes a level of fascinating discussion that the book itself doesn't really earn or deserve. That is, it's more interesting to talk about the book and its implications than to actually read it.<br /><br />It would surprise me if this were the first book to adopt an autistic person's point of view, but that seems to be what has everyone talking. My issue is that this tact presents some formidable challenges to any writer who attempts it, and I don't think Haddon really succeeds. For example, one of the main traits of an autistic person is a lack of empathy; they have a difficult time connecting with others. That doesn't mean they don't have feelings, because they certainly do, but they lack the ability to recognize those feelings in others or to express their own feelings in ways appropriate to the non-autistic world. This all makes someone like Christopher a difficult character to like, at least in the context of a novel. Of course I'm not saying that it's impossible to like or love an actual autistic person. But as written in this book, I found it ultimately too trying to empathize with a person who displays no empathy, to the point that he appears quite cruel in certain moments. This in itself is very interesting; is it possible to empathize with someone who cannot empathize with you? Would that violate the definition of \\"empathy\\"? Or does empathy possess some kind of transitive property?<br /><br />The author often makes pit stops, interrupting the narrative to have Christopher explain what preoccupies his thoughts, mostly because he retreats into his own complex inner world in times of stress or fear. Christopher is fascinated by math and logic most of all, so at many times the book digresses to explain a math problem or logical riddle, complete with diagrams. At first this seems refreshing, an interesting departure from traditional narrative writing, but as the book goes on it becomes suspect and tiresome. Maybe this is how the author intended to instill empathy for Christopher in the reader - as if these things would make us think, \\"What interesting thoughts Christopher has beneath his difficult exterior - what a neat kid!\\" It didn't work like that for me. As more and more of these episodes pile up, it begins to seem cheap and gimmicky, as if Haddon is simply using the stereotypical autistic preoccupation with math and logic as an excuse to thrill the reader with neat facts, stories and riddles, the kinds of things that might appear as sidebars in a science magazine. It takes on a too-cute quality, and in the last third of the book, it even becomes kind of maddening. Important things are happening to Christopher, and he keeps interrupting the flow of the plot to retreat into more of these math problems. It stops being interesting and seems superfluous and almost disrespectful of the reader - a kind of crude and taunting way to build suspense.<br /><br />Autistic people tend to be quite literal and pedantic when they speak or explain things, and Christopher is this way throughout the book. His voice as a narrator is extremely pedantic, and like the little logic/math vignettes I describe above, this got increasingly old for me as the book went on. This is one of the challenges I mentioned, that I don't think Haddon quite rose up to: how do you accurately portray a character who thinks and speaks pedantically, without alienating or irritating the reader at some point?<br /><br />Another challenge in this book: how does the writer make subtext and emotional content apparent to the reader, when the voice of the book is unaware of these things? Sometimes Haddon succeeds in this, and these are the most potent moments of the book. Yes, at times there are impactful flashes of emotion and complex sadnessthat surface. In the first half of the book I felt conflicted sympathy for Christopher's father, and his mother and their trials. But there's not enough of this stuff shining through the superficially obtuse, cutesy, pedantic way Haddon chooses to present Christopher's voice. The plot of the book itself is pretty simplistic, and plays it safe. In the end, I feel Haddon was never aiming that high to begin with. I feel he began with a novel concept - why not write a story from an autistic point of view? - and he did just that, but *only* that. He didn't sort out some of the messier by-products of that approach. He tells a simple story with a novel point of view and uses the autistic conceit to present us with some \\"interesting\\" science vignettes along the way. That just isn't enough, not for me.<br /><br />I think this would have worked better as a longish short story or novella; limiting the length would have prevented the above problems from being such an issue. Perhaps he could have alternated Christopher's point of view with his father's point of view? That might have been pretty great, actually. As it is, this book doesn't handle things as well as similar novels like Flowers for Algernon or Forrest Gump. Like \\"Life of Pi,\\" it's a long and problematic children's story masquerading as adult popular literature.

B000002JSJ|3.0|Presence represents Zeppelin past their creative peak, but the group still manage to whip out a collection of songs that are quite solid and still contain alot of ambient power and majesty that recalls some of their former glory days. Opening with Achilles Last Stand, Jimmy Page's craft of overdubbing his guitar melodies is, once more, to the fore here and builds this number along with the powerful kit playing of Bonham, John Paul Jones' steady bass and Robert Plant's familiar vocal voicings which play off of Page further into the duration of the number. For Your Life is a mid-paced track that has a touch of blues and a slow boogie rhythm attached to its arrangement, but also provides space for Page to stretch out and the track benefits from his invigorous guitar lines. Royal Orleans is a bit more uptempo, but still retains a boogie feel and lyrically was inspired by John Paul Jones' trip to the cajun country infamous French Quarter in New Orleans; its slightly derivative, but is a bit of fun for the band as the feel is musically loose, but also driven by the power of Bonham's drumming.<br /><br />Nobody's Fault But Mine retreads the familiar blues base of what made Zeppelin such an important rock institution while also showing off the core of the group's collective powers amongst Page's ambient guitars, Plant's intertwined voicings and Bonham duly accentuating the track's stop-start rhythm patterns. The track would feature along with Achilles' as more of the prominent features on the subsquent '77 and '79 touring shows and provides Zep with stronger latter day material to mix within the setlists. Candy Store Rock has a somewhat rockabilly rave-up quality about it and has a touch of filler within the song cycle of the record. Hots on for Nowhere echcoes a bit of the style of Royal Orleans, previously, and has a bouncy melodic vibe about it that makes for a refreshing listen and further drives the vibe of the record which, taken in overall, brings Presence as a simple and basic release that strips down the sometimes, overblown arrangements that adorned prior Zep albums like Houses of the Holy and Physical Graffiti. Tea for One is the record's closer, and once again, Zep conjures up a longer track that returns to the blues for inspiration and although it features more blistering leads from Page, it is also a track that is seemingly lost in the shuffle of quality Zeppelin material, oftentimes looked over, and when it is discovered, becomes a powerful unsung hero in the Zeppelin catalogue.<br /><br />I find Presence as a satisfying release and while its clear that the band had peaked creatively on the hodge-podge double set Physical Graffiti, Presence shows the pure muscle and heart of the band even though the material may be berefit of quality songwriting to match the instrumentation involved. Still, Presence contains a slow, brimming power to it that can ultimately draw you into the simplistic, yet solid qualitiies of the aura of an aging, but still pioneering group of its time. 3 and a half stars.

B0024RID6U|4.0|Tattoo You represents a mini-renaissance for the Stones here and even though the majority of the songs stem from outtakes(some go as far back as Goats Head Soup ala Tops), the overall presentation sounds crisp, clear and fresh. Start Me Up provided the band with another anthem to add to the canon of classic material and features an archetypal Richards riff that is instantaneously familiar and profound, coupled with Charlie Watts' mechanical drum beat and the gloriously sloppy harmonies from Jagger, Richards and Wood. Hang Fire is another Chuck Berryesque rocker which features sunny harmonies and biting leads from Ronnie which makes this track succeed most tellingly. Slave dates from Black and Blue and is a funky rocker that lets the Stones jam out a little bit coupled with some great sax work from Sonny Rollins, whom also adds his talents to two other numbers in Neighbors(one of 2 new tracks written for this record) and the album's closer, the carefree and laidback comfort of Waiting on a Friend.<br /><br />Little T&A features Keith on lead vocals and like Happy before it, it is a good old sleaze rocker that's rough around the edges and cooks its way through as an overlooked gem from the band and for Richards in particular. Black Limousine is a derivative blues vamp that chugs along nicely and features a piercing wailing harp accompaniment from Jagger. Worried About You begins with Electric piano and is another Black and Blue outtake that avoids oversentimental traits and succeeds as a midpaced track for the album. The aforementioned Tops came under scrutiny many years later by one Mick Taylor, whom had claimed that the finished track was used on Tattoo You and the Stones failed to acknowledge and credit Taylor for his guitar contributions to the finished track; all parties have since settled the slight and Tops is a track that continues in the vein of the previous Worried About You in its midpaced, somewhat soul flavor. Heaven is the second and final \\"new\\" track tobe included and uses studio trickery and mixing which gives this number an ethereal and dreamlike quality in its structure. As a whole, I find Tattoo You along with Some Girls and to a slight extent, Undercover as being a loose triad of solid and sometimes powerful moments that revealed the Stones in their older age as still remaining somewhat relevant after all these years and still maintaining a strong finger on the pulse for their latter day era. 4 stars

Before we had an inkling of the global financial crises starting in 2007, Taleb, with almost impeccable timing, discusses the risks inherent in the big banks, and goes on to say, \\"Likewise the government sponsored institution Fanny Mae, when I look at their risks, seems to be sitting on a barrel of dynamite,..\\". I should say, he wrote the book in 2006 and published in 2007.<br /><br />I, like many others, would ask, why isn't this required reading for those thinking of wanting a life in politics, economics and/or finance.<br /><br />The Black Swan challenges our desire and ability to acknowledge the impact of unknown, highly improbable events that can and will occur over our life-times looking at some of the features of human psychology that make us poor at evaluating uncertainties and risks, instead, amongst other things relying on narrative explanations and blinkered views.<br /><br />In \\"The Black Swan,\\" Taleb proclaims that the unexpected is the key to understanding not just financial markets but history itself. History, he writes, proceeds by \\"jumps,\\" controlled by \\"the tyranny of the singular, the accidental, the unseen and the unpredicted.<br /><br />Whilst the book contains numerous insights evidenced by examples such as: \\"We attribute our successes to our skills, and our failures to external events outside our control.\\" Like all things, there are shortcomings. The bulk of this book is underpinned by criticizing others for failing to see the future -- though who exactly can see the future? And on this Taleb generalizes, for instance scoffing that the collapse of the Soviet Union was an unfathomably unlikely event that \\"no social scientist saw coming.\\" The historian John Mueller's brilliant 1989 book, \\"Retreat From Doomsday,\\" published just before the Berlin Wall fell, did see it coming.<br /><br />The Black Swan is brilliant, thought-provoking and challenging in the most constructive and valuable ways.an John Mueller's brilliant 1989 book, \\"Retreat From Doomsday,\\" published just before the Berlin Wall fell, did see it coming.<br /><br />The Black Swan is brilliant, thought-provoking and challenging in the most constructive and valuable ways.

0345803507|3.0|The entire series is a perfectly good fluff piece. This one was my favorite of the three, so I'll just review this one.<br /><br />Yes, I know that it was fanfiction first, which it does read like. The way the first book ends is exactly the way a fanfiction writer ends a story they're about to have a sequel to.  The first book especially is laden with bizarre consistency errors (7 hour of sleep goes to 8, or particularly bad is a scene where Ana starts out wearing a mint skirt, and ends wearing jeans).  Also, it's not very well written, though the author herself acknowledges this and can't seem to believe that people like it so much.<br /><br />Yes, I know it's based of Twilight, but while certain character dynamics are the same, it's really not very like Twilight... at all. I actually preferred this series to Twilight (and no, it's not all the kinky, kinky sex. Smut bores me terribly, and I ended up skimming just about every scene where they have sex.) What I liked better about this series is that I actually like the main couple. I never hated any of Twilight's characters, but Bella was not someone I liked, and I never liked Edward at all. In contrast I actually like Ana and Christian. Ana is actually not useless, and when the time comes she is very capable and resourceful (though I wish it had shown up before the third book instead of just being talked about).<br /><br />No, Ana is not a strong heroine, and no, Christian is not an ideal boyfriend and their relationship is weird and shouldn't work, but somehow it does. No, do not take this for real life... but then who actually think that real life is supposed to be like this? There's no reason to call this a blow to feminism. It's a book, it's fluff, it's fun.<br /><br />Why did I like this book the most? Because at some point there's an actual threat. The first book was the getting to know each other book. Book two seemed to drag on... a lot. I kept wondering why nothing was happening... or why the main action ofthe book seemed to be wrapped up half way through. This one seemed to have the same problem as book two, until I got to the end and some action really did happen and I ended up ignoring the rest of the world for about an hour and a half so I could finish it all. Also, this book has the part at the end from Christian's perspective, which I found really cute and interesting.<br /><br />Now, a bit on the characters. In one of the reviews someone complained that college students don't think the way Ana does, that her thinking is very adolescent, like a high school girl. As a college student about Ana's age I can say that her thinking is really pretty normal. Most college students see themselves more as kids than as adults, even being able to drink and drive and vote (though not all at the same time, we should hope). Bella Swan felt like a teenage girl, with all the whining and teen angst. Yes, this has whining and angst, but the character is self aware enough to reflect on her own actions and things she does.<br /><br />She's not my favorite character ever, but as an avatar she does quiet well and as a character she stands on her own fine. Christian Grey is an appealing man. Women like a project. He's also messed up enough that you can see that Ana is also messed up. There's a moment in the second book when Dr. Flynn points out that her self esteem is almost as bad as Christian's is. With Bella Swan, her comments on her looks never made me question that she was plain, which made me question why Edward even cared except that she smelled nice. Ana is shown to be a bit romantic, a bit spacey, and most of all and English major, someone so wrapped up in her own world that she hardly notices the outside. I believe that she doesn't realize that she's being flirted with, because it's just a function of that particular mindset (speaking from experience, and knowing from other English majors).<br /><br />The question is, is the story any good? It's not great, and it's not Twilight. There's not as much action, but I cared more about the characters than I did with Twilight. I did want to see what would happen to them. One of my favorite things about the story is just seeing the intimacy. Normally stories with romances end once the couple gets together, but the couple sustains three books by being together the whole time, just learning about each other. Things are a bit repetitive, but then people like to use the same jokes over and over, and they need the same reassurances. Someone telling you that you're beautiful once will never cut it, and for two people who think badly of themselves positive reassurance is pretty necessary.<br /><br />On the whole I can see why people love these books: they're fun. They're not great story telling, but they're enjoyable and relaxing and just gripping enough to be interesting without forcing the reader to abandon their lives to finish the series. Not good, not bad, but definitely fun.

0802798225|5.0|Originally Reviewed at:Mother/Gamer/Writer<br />Rating: 5 out of 5 Controllers<br />Review Source: Purchased<br />Reviewer:Jennifer<br /><br />Perfect Chemistry by Simone Elkeles is a new and interesting take on the phrase; opposites attract. This is the tale of two seemingly different teenagers from two very different worlds on the north and south sides of suburban Chicago. This tale narrated by its two main characters, Alejandro Fuentes and Brittany Ellis.<br /><br />Brittany Ellis is a typical high school student living in the wealthy north side of suburban Chicago. To her friends and other school mates, she has the perfect life, perfect family, perfect car, and perfect boyfriend, but this is a myth that she encourages to disguise the truth. At home, if Brittany doesn't do exactly as her mother wishes, she has the deal with her mother's intense attitude and often yelling. Her father offers no relief as he is always gone with his job at a computer company. On the few occasions' that he is home, he does not interact with the family choosing to lock himself away in his office and continue his work. Brittany's only relief comes from caring for her elder sister Shelly. Shelly, who has cerebral palsy, cannot function very well on her own and must be taken care of 24/7. Spending time with Shelly is the Brittany's only real relief from the stress of her chaotic life. The chaos only worsens when she catches the eye of well known Latin Blood member Alejandro Fuentes.<br /><br />Alejandro Fuentes is an eighteen year old high school student living with his mother and two younger brothers on the south side of the same city. Because his father was murdered when Alex was very young, responsibility has fallen on his shoulders to be the man of the house. This includes joining the Latin Bloods in order to keep his family safe. This also means making sure that his younger brothers, fifteen year old Carlos and eleven year old Luis, away from the same lifestyle. Though Alex does achieve high grades and has dreams of going to college and having a better life, he feels that he will never achieve those dreams. Therefore, he intends to see that his brother can have the life that he can't. Much like the locations, his life is the exact opposite of Brittany's. At least that's what they believe.<br /><br />Brittany and Alex's paths first cross on the first day of their senior year at Fairfield High School when Brittany nearly hits Alex when attempting to pull into the same parking space. Their paths cross again when the two are paired together in their chemistry class leading them into a relationship neither can understand nor stop.<br /><br />Simone Elkeles does a phenomenal job capturing the reality of teenage relationships and situations. She keeps the book interesting by putting the reality of teenage life into a romance novel for young adults. She also tells of the realities of gang life and how seemingly perfect lives aren't always what they seem. 0071636080|5.0|Disclaimer: I am an Apple lover and a big fan of Steve Jobs, very grateful and appreciative of products that I have found to be transformative.  I greatly respect his dedication to the passionate pursuit of excellence.<br /><br />I have always admired the oratorical skills of Steve Jobs, particularly the brilliant Apple keynote speeches and his iconic commencement address at Stanford.  I do<br />my fair share of public speaking and aspire to have the smooth style, brevity and enthusiasm that are intrinsic to the grand master.  Carmine Gallo does an inspiring job in deconstructing the key elements of the Jobs' oratorical techniques so as to understand how to deliver a phenomenal presentation.  This book was an easy and interesting read and I found it to be of great utility and immediately integrated the tenets and secrets of Steve Job's rhetorical skills and passionate style into my own public addresses, whether the audience be one person or an auditorium-full of people.  In the following words, I will summarize the salient points made by Carmine Gallo.<br /><br />Regarding the use of data during a presentation, make it specific, relevant, and contextual. Speak in simple, clear, and direct language.  Unclutter and eliminate redundant language, buzzwords, and jargon. Edit, edit, and edit some more.  Gallo references Kawasaki's qualities of an outstanding demonstration: simple, short, sweet, swift and substantial.<br /><br />Make an effort to stroke the dopamine receptors of your audience. After all, your customers are your sales force and the most potent evangelists for your cause.<br />Dopamine greatly aids memory and information processing and if you can get the brain to put a chemical \\"post-it\\" note on an idea, it will be more robustly processed and easily remembered.  Powerful terms, when integrated them into your talks, will help release the dopamine, as will an emotionally charged delivery. SJ loved the following zippy words: amazing; incredible; gorgeous; insanely great; coolest; buckle up; put on your shoulder harness; lust object; stunning; miraculously engineered.<br /><br />Presentation skills are fundamental.  It is important to maintain good eye contact and positive body language: an open body without fidgeting or other distracting habits without looking back at slides or hiding behind the lectern. The delivery should vary the vocal volume, inflection, and cadence.  Filler words should be avoided and pauses should be used in their place.  Unleash your inner Zen by using very few words and plenty of compelling visuals.  An energetic delivery is quintessential--passion in the voice, a bounce in the step, and a smile on the face are inspirational.  Enthusiasm makes one likable.  Share your passion for your, subject and your enthusiasm will be contagious.<br />One's speaking style should be informal and casual. Never read slides or turn your back to the audience.  Slides should be highly visual with one key idea only.  Memorize the one key idea per slide. Practice the entire presentation without notes simply using slides as prompter. Spontaneity is the result of planned practice!<br /><br />Steve Jobs consistently adheres to the rule of three:  the human mind can only consume three points of information in short-term memory. Our brains crave meaning before detail, so deliver the big picture before filling in the details. If you can't describe your product or service in 140 characters or less, go back to the drawing board. Every great book or movie has a hero and a villain. Consider a presentation in the same way--a theatrical event complete with a protagonist and antagonist.  Jobs uses the rhetorical device of raising a question and providing the answer. Your audience is asking what's in it for me?  Don't leave them guessing. The villain can be a competitor or in many cases a problem in need of a solution.<br />Most presenters have more information than they can easily convey in a short amount of time. Don't try to squeeze in everything. Simplify communications. If you want to deliver a Jobs-worthy presentation, avoid content overload.  The 10-minute rule simply states that your audience will lose attention after 10 minutes.  At the 10-minute mark introduce a break in the action: a video, stories, another speaker, and a demo all can be effective. A prop is anything to take the attention away from the presentation and gives the audience a break from the slides. They appreciate the diversion.<br /><br />In summary, Carmine Gallo's book is a winner and will prove to be of great help in improving the communication skills of anybody doing a presentation. I only wish that the presenters at many of the medical lectures that I have attended had read this book!<br /><br />Andrew Siegel, M.D.<br />AUTHOR OF: PROMISCUOUS EATING--UNDERSTANDING AND ENDING OUR SELF-DESTRUCTIVE RELATIONSHIP WITH FOODeze in everything. B000HWZ5W4|5.0|Robert Plant found his new (true?)identity on this album.  He wasn't the cock rocker from the 70's anymore with that 'other' band, and he was settled in a new phase of his life: solo artist and single.  His first solo album was somewhat transitional, sounding a lot like LZ and occasionally touching on his divorce.<br /><br />In this album, he is more reflective and exploratory - both in the music and the lyrics.  I've always felt this album was the perfect Robert Plant vehicle.  He is a singer, first and foremost, and this album let's that golden voice take the spotlight.  The music is indeed 'ominous melancholy\\" and is constructed in a way to be non-obstructive.  Basically, a subdued (not including the rocker 'Wreckless Love') and atmospheric sonic backdrop was created to act as a platform for Robert's voice to 'walk around' on: there are hills and valleys, strange stairs that climb and drop, and the mood is consistent throughout.<br /><br />Robert's voice has never sounded better (well ... maybe on the Honeydrippers album but that's another review), and as others have mentioned in their reviews, 'Big Log' is a highlight.  His vocals on this song are the stuff dreams are made of.  Other highlights are 'Thru with the Two Step' and the wonderful and very 80's 'In the Mood'.<br /><br />When I first heard this album, I was in high school and loved it.  As I got older, and went through my share of relationships, I 'got' this album a lot more as there really is a lot of relationship-oriented stuff on here, both pre and post divorce in Plant's life.<br /><br />While this album is definitely a grower, it is more than worth every one of the five stars I gave it.  Essential listening.

0374203032|2.0|I'm afraid this book is likely to give \\"moral philosophers\\" a bad name. And it isn't simply because the dust jacket uses the \\"bait and switch\\" technique of praise: big names are trotted out to commend the author on the strength of another - excellent - book: Justice, rather than this far less valuable effort. The current product reads like a collation of excerpts from term papers the author has gathered in his undergraduate course, lightly mixed with thin moral sauce.<br /><br />Take the first section: \\"Jumping the queue\\". Out of 21 pages, about 16 are forgettable anecdotes repeating the same point; the rest is reflection. The author misses the fundamental point: queues are more a market than a moral mechanism. The \\"currency\\" in this kind of market is the disutility of having to wait in line. One can legitimately either charge a price, or ask people to queue. When both pricing mechanisms are made to coexist, however, trouble starts: the currency of \\"queing\\" is debased and it no longer works - consumers no longer are treated equally (a necessity for a well-functioning market). It's like bi-metallism: parallel currencies are incompatible with each other and create havoc at speculators try to arbitrage. Collaterally one may ask - strictly in market terms - why should a third party (the scalper, the firm that hires place-keepers in the queue) be allowed to skim off \\"consumer surplus\\"? To do so is to pervert the functioning of markets, which reject monopolies, even third party ones. Again, the matter needs no morals, only insights in how a market works, or fails.<br /><br />On the other hand the author misses the social and political implications of queuing to participate in a Congressional hearing. These hearings are one of the few places left for Mr. Public to make his concerns known to the Legislative, if nothing else by showing up. Packing the hearing with paid partisanship lowers the democratic debate. Atmospherics count, and Congressmen need also to feel the \\"willof the people\\" rather than just the \\"will of the lobbies\\".<br /><br />The second section, on \\"incentives\\", is even worse. The author misses the whole point of \\"incentives\\": incentives are a half-way house between regulation and indifference of the legislator (see: [[ASIN:014311526X Nudge: Improving Decisions About Health, Wealth, and Happiness]]). They are indicative rather than prescriptive, and allow the person to adapt and adjust. There is much merit in \\"nudging\\" people, rather than forcing them to comply: it allows for flexibility and exceptions. Nudging also lowers compliance costs significantly - all points worth considering.<br /><br />What the author also misses - and this is a core point - is the fact that \\"economics\\" is not so much about dollars and cents as about choices in a material context. The author cites Gary Becker disapprovingly for stating: \\"I have come to the position that the economic approach is a comprehensive one that is applicable to all human behavior...\\" (pg. 49). But Gary Becker is right. If I have one op-room and two very sick patients, I must make choices and tradeoffs and so much moralism about \\"life being invaluable\\" will not help. How best to call this judgment is a matter of proper balancing of material as well as moral benefits and costs - one that an ethical committee of MDs will be familiar with. And irreconcilable material considerations will be in the forefront. Over time, coherence and consistence are needed - on moral grounds. All of this is the stuff of properly understood \\"economics\\" - the art of balancing considerations.<br /><br />The author finally misses two fundamental points about markets. Markets are based on legal contracts - signposts established by society and determining the conditions for transactions. The corrosive effect of the market ideology arises when moving the legal signposts can become a matter for negotiation in accordance with \\"benefits and costs\\". It is a well-known economic theorem that any \\"constraint\\" reduces profits, so de-regulation (moving or eliminating goal-posts) axiomatically means more profits. Deregulation always \\"pays\\". But when there are no rules left, the gun rules - naked power. Markets emerge through social institutions, yet undermine them. Politically countervailing forces are needed to keep it from destroying society and in the end, itself.<br /><br />The other core issue has to do with division of labor, on which markets are based. Division of labor puts the individual into a schizophrenic stance. In a minimal area he is pro-active and professional; the rest of the time, and in particular his private life, he accepts passively the expertise of other professionals. \\"Think locally\\" - is the mantra. This is abdication from responsibility for the whole - be it for himself or society - and the beginning of the end.<br /><br />PS: Jeremy WALDRON has published a review of this book in the August 16 issue of the New York Review of Books. Though more guarded is his formulations it appears to me that he is making essentially similar points. He notes \\"over 100 examples\\" - my point that in this aspect may be overrepresented. He comments \\"I think [SANDEL] wants to open the discussion up rather than nail it down\\". Between a simple \\"opening\\" and the ultimative \\"nailing it down\\" there is, however, the \\"framing the discussion\\" in such a way as to facilitate rational analysis. WALDRON points to the seminal work of Michael WALZER [[ASIN:0465081894 Spheres Of Justice: A Defense Of Pluralism And Equality]], which SANDEL has reviewed [[ASIN:067402365X Public Philosophy: Essays on Morality in Politics]], but failed to engage in this latest offering. A pity.

B006P0FD4W|4.0|It must be said that the \\"found footage\\" sub-genre of horror movies requires the audience to suspend disbelief a bit.  The idea is clever - you're watching something that allegedly really happened, which gives the movie a bit of documentary-style credibility.  But you have to accept that at some point in the movie, you're going to shout, \\"put down the darn camera!\\"  It's surprising that the \\"Scream\\" franchise hasn't mocked this trope yet.<br /><br />Either you can get past this, or you can't.  If you can, the \\"Paranormal Activity\\" series should be on your must-see list.  The first two movies tell the present-day stories of Kristi and Katie, two sisters whose pleasantly suburban lifestyles are ripped asunder by a sinister spectral force.  In \\"Paranormal Activity 3,\\" we return to 1988, where the sisters first meet the poltergeist who will terrify them and their families a few years down the road.<br /><br />The movie sets the stage by showing grown-up Kristy and Katie rediscovering old videotapes from when they were children - a gift from recently departed Grandma.  These tapes are later stolen in a scene all too familiar with fans of the first two films.  The movie then switches to the videotapes.<br /><br />Young Katie and Kristi are enjoying Katie's birthday party, and we also meet their mom Julie and her boyfriend Dennis.  (Oddly, there's never any mention of the girls' biological father - where did he go . . . hmmm.)  Dennis, conveniently for the movie's conceit, is a videographer, so naturally his response to every development is his life is to tape it.  During the birthday video, we learn that Kristi has a close relationship with an imaginary friend, Toby.<br /><br />Toby, as you might guess, turns out to be more than imaginary, and he quickly proves to be one scary, demanding guy.  To avoid spoilers, that's it for the plot summary.<br /><br />Directors Henry Joost and Ariel Schulman know how to make a found-footage movie.  Tension builds nicely as we strain towatch a seemingly innocuous scene - are the bedsheets moving?  How about the lamp?  Perhaps the creepiest scenes (before the final 15 minutes, which are terrifying) come with their use of a video camera set up on an oscillating fan base so the camera slowly pans from one part of the house to another - the directors' clever use of the \\"that freaking thing wasn't there a few seconds ago\\" trick will have you looking over your own shoulder the next time you're all alone in the house and hear an unexplained noise.<br /><br />For a third installment in a horror series, \\"Paranormal Activity 3\\" exceeds all expectations.  A spooky film that builds to a truly frightening third act, \\"3\\" succeeds in deepening the story created by the first two installments without wearing out our interest - the filmmakers know that we're here to get scared, not to get a PhD-level understanding of Toby . . . and scare you they do.  Terrific stuff.

B003TOH8AE|4.0|These days, it feels like you can't go 3 months without a movie based on a comic coming out. Usually they're the superhero kind, and other times you get something completely different like Oldboy or Sin City. I've never been a big fan of the superhero genre of comics or movies, apart from the ones that were meant to be entertaining and no much else. Kick-Ass didn't seem very interesting when I saw the trailer for it, and I hadn't read the comic either. I just watched the movie last night, and I think I'll be ordering the comic pretty soon because the movie sure won me over. Kick-Ass has just about everything I want in a good movie, and while there were two things toward the end that killed the moment for me, I have to say that it's one of the better movies I've seen this year.<br /><br />Dave Lizewski is your average high school geek. He has no luck with the ladies, fantasizes about his [totally stacked] teacher Mrs. Zane, isn't even the funny one in his small group of friends, and spends most of his time at the comic shop or online. To make matters worse, he's constantly being robbed by two hoodlums in the neighborhood. During one robbing session, he notices someone observing the situation from their window. Rather than come down and help or call the police, the person does nothing, and this irritates Dave to the point of wanting to make a difference. He orders a scuba diver suit that looks kinda superhero-y, gets a baton for a weapon, and makes it his goal to become a local superhero: Kick-Ass. And wouldn't you know it, he just so happens to encounter the hoodlums pretty soon after getting his outfit. Things...don't go as well as he'd like, though he was off to a great start, and Dave gets stabbed AND run over, accidentally, by someone else who just drives off without helping him. The poor kid ends up in the hospital and comes out with messed up nerve endings and some enhanced bones. Rather than give up, he continues with the Kick-Ass thing and when he finally manages to bring justice to someone being beat up by 3 big dudes, it gets recorded and goes up on the internet, making him an instant star. But at the same time, there's a rumor going around of someone who looks like Batman...or just a superhero...messing up a local mob kingpin's business. Yep, Kick-Ass is in over his head, and it's going to take some people with much better training than him to set things right.<br /><br />I was impressed by how well comedy, drama and action were all mixed together in Kick-Ass. The first quarter of the movie is comical, the middle is action-packed, and the final part has a lot of action with drama while still knowing when to throw in some humor. And my god was I happy to see that they had him fighting like someone who didn't know what they were doing and just brawling to their heart's content. I hate fights in movies that -look- like choreographed fights (think Tron Legacy's god-awful bar/club fight), but nearly everything here looked good. Even more amazing was Nicholas Cage giving a performance that wasn't bad. In fact, this could be the best thing I've seen him do in years. He does a terrific job as the Big Daddy character: a nice blend of loving father and revenge-driven psycho. Who knew? But, I have to agree with the majority here, when I say that the real winner of the movie is Hit-Girl. A little girl who isn't even a tween, cussing up a storm and literally shredding bad guys to bits with double bladed staffs, knives and more. Somehow, her character fits into the world of Kick-Ass, which sounds silly on paper but when you see it for yourself, it works. Had the movie been just about Kick-Ass himself, it probably would've gotten stale with the whole awkward superhero thing. Leave it to Big Daddy and Hit-Girl to keep things moving along just nicely. There isn't much else I can rave about without ruining some plot points. The cast and acting is solid all-around, even from Mark Strong and Mr. McLovin.<br /><br />It's too bad that thefinal act is, in my eyes, underwhelming thanks to the big reveal of something Big Daddy and Hit-Girl order. Not that what I was expecting was much better, but when it's showtime, my shoulders drooped and I was let down severely. The climax is ruined even more by the use of a weapon. I won't say what, but when it's used, man...had that happened earlier on, I might have just turned the movie off because it was such an easy way to end things. So much for a big finish. And yes, I know this is a comic book movie, so it might've worked better in the comic, but on the big screen it looks goofy. Sorry.<br /><br />Despite the below average climax, I had a good time watching it, and will be buying it sometime in the future. Kick-Ass deserves the money it earned in theaters (nearly 100m worldwide), and earns the R rating it has. How often do you see an action movie that could've been PG-13 if not for a few profanities? If you're sick of Spider-Man or like your heroes a little more realistic, go with Kick-Ass.<br /><br />Once again, big props to Mrs. Zane.

0441013597|4.0|I've been meaning to read Frank Herbert's Dune for years now because I love sci-fi/fantasy and because I feel it's important to understand how a genre will forever be influenced by one of its signature works. The world of Dune (both the planet and the larger fictional universe) is notable for its rich detail of culture, history and mythology. Herbert probably borrowed from Tolkein when he added the supplementary glossary and appendix, which I flipped to as often as I encountered a strange word or custom.<br /><br />Dune was first published in 1965 and while many of its themes endure to this day, I believe the story says so much more about the decade in which it was written. Environmentalism was gaining momentum as new movement. The hugely popular Lawrence of Arabia film was released just three years prior. The myth of the messianic off-worlder redeeming a tribal culture might seem overdone in today's fantasy novels, but that's because Dune and its contemporaries told that story so well.

B007AJFSJW|5.0|With his latest book Nobel laureate economist Paul Krugman boldly claims that the American economy is in a depression, not a recession and that, despite protestations from politicians and fellow economists, we have the power to end this depression, but we lack the political leadership to do so.  The first portion of Krugman's argument is provocative enough and certainly spurs disputation with his fellow macroeconomists.  Some, like Krugman, agree that we've been in a prolonged recession since 2008 with brief periods of recovery followed by relapses.  By now most everyone knows what brought on our current economic malaise: consumers became over-leveraged with debt, our government ran massive deficits, and a housing bubble was allowed to develop unchecked and subsequently burst.  When the housing market crashed and we all realized how overextended we all were the economy went into an intense period of retrenchment from which we have not yet recovered.  Krugman offers up a summary of the crisis in a way that lay people can understand.  Macroeconomics may be boring to some, but it's not impenetrable, and Krugman walks readers through it all with concise easy to follow prose.  The parallels to the Great Depression are a bit ominous to say the least, especially if you've studied that period of our history.  The economists and the public of that era scarcely understood what was occurring and were uncertain how to progress.  Much like our current era there were points where the economy seemed to recover.  Certainly not everyone was unemployed and economic activity continued on at varying levels, just as now.  And that's the scary part, the parallels between the Great Depression and now that Krugman lays out.  The three legs of the economy, spending by consumers, businesses, and government, all gave out at roughly the same time.  An admitted Keynesian economist, Krugman argues that government must step in to prop up the economy, even if we are carrying a burdensome debt load, and that this stimulus can only be provided so long as the economy is sluggish and until consumer and business spending recovers.<br /><br />Krugman's invocation of Keynesian economic policy is likely to be the most controversial argument as macroeconomists remain divided on the wisdom of Keynesian economic policy.  The massive debt racked up by the U.S. government over the past decade makes arguing for increased deficit spending a hard case to advocate for.  The rise of the Tea Party and deficit hawks complicates things further.  The polarization of our two party system and an unwillingness to find common cause further poisons the atmosphere.  Complicating things further is the general publics and politicians general and seemingly willful ignorance of the principles of macroeconomics renders them unable to comprehend what is happening and how to respond; a situation which again mirrors the 1930s.  What is truly frightening is that political leaders from Federal Reserve Chairman Paul Bernanke (a scholar on the Great Depression for heaven's sake!), to the President, to members of Congress don't seem to be able to grasp even the basics of macroeconomics and how to respond to a crisis, a theme addressed quite in Robert Draper's [[ASIN:1451642083 Do Not Ask What Good We Do: Inside the U.S. House of Representatives]], which I recommend and read while reading this book.  The forced austerity of Greece, the United Kingdom, Spain, Portugal, and Ireland should all point to the danger of cutting off government spending at a time when consumer and business spending have been deeply entrenched, and yet we are failing to head the warning signs.  The United Kingdom's recent slide into yet another recession should be the warning sign for deficit hawks that now is not the time to cut government spending, but again that is an oversimplification.  The United Kingdom has a harder time offering treasury bonds and notes in the global market, something the United States is not hamperedby.  The question is how long will that last?<br /><br />Krugman makes points that are by turns frightening, accurate, and frustrating.  Pointing out the similarities to the Great Depression is a bit frightening as we linger in the fourth year of our current economic malaise.  Some may argue that hypothesis as there are certainly bright spots to our economy, but if you were to read the newspapers and histories of the 1930s you'd see the same thing occurring then.  It's clear that now as then we're fumbling our way through a crisis with halfhearted measures, avoiding some of the clear mistakes of the 1930s but sadly repeating others.  Readers may agree or disagree with Krugman, but at least he's consistent in his beliefs and able to articulate them in a way that is approachable and understandable.  Clearly Krugman is pitching this idea with an eye on the 2012 elections.  Whether his ideas resonate with voters and more importantly politicians remains to be seen.  I cannot help of the Rooseveltian idea of at least trying SOMETHING to end our malaise.by. B0071XO8RA|4.0|Proving that bargains *can* still be found in the digital books sector despite price collusion allegations agains the Big 6 and Apple, this Omnibus edition of Wool is currently selling on Amazon for the measly price of 4.99. This novel (which is a collection of 5 installments - almost like chapbooks from ye olden days) clocks in at a whopping 550 pages, but quality was not sacrificed for quantity, and Howey proves that despite the pounds of dregs found upon the self-publishing mire that is the Amazon Kindle store there are still gems to be found by authors that show a lot of promise.<br /><br />Despite the fact that all 5 of these installments are available individually for .99  to 2.99 each (depending on the length of the chapter), I'll be reviewing the omnibus edition because honestly I think that if you are going to start reading this series you'll want to buy the whole thing, it's really worth it.<br /><br />The first installment is obviously a short story - a good one, but a short story nonetheless, which opens the scene for the reader of where the story takes place - a post apocalyptic world, destroyed and ravaged beyond belief, where people live in Silos that span hundreds of levels underground. Those that transgress against the laws of the silos, that go stir crazy and wish to escape, are sent out for the 'cleaning', a veritable death sentence where they sacrifice themselves for the good of the thousands that remain, leaving the safe confines of the Silo in a badly created hazmat suit to 'clean' the cameras that allow for the only views of the outside world available.<br /><br />There are some pacing issues in the first few installments which I felt were due to the episodic nature that the author chose for his tale - Wool 2, for instance, was a bit of a slog to get through. However, I did feel that the choices were understandable and perhaps even a self-aware choice to make the reader feel the same way as the protagonists as they make they way through hundreds of levels of stairs.<br /><br />By the time you reach Wool 4 and 5 - which are the longest of the chapter installments - all pacing issues are long gone and the reader is in for a wild ride as pieces fall into place and all the story building reaches their inevitable conclusions.<br /><br />I enjoyed this post apocalyptic world that Howey created, and readers who also enjoy video games will no doubt be reminded of the Fallout series. Howey obviously put in a lot of time and effort to creating the world of the Silo, with its multitudinous levels, each one an entity on its own with a specific purpose to the greater good, from nurseries to machinery, and the all powerful 'IT department'.<br /><br />I look forward to reading more stories set in this world and finding out more about what caused the disasters to happen. I think it was a bold and wise choice for Howey to hold back some of the mysteries and not reveal all too quickly. I was definitely impressed by this effort by a self-published author and I'm sure I'll be checking out more of his workds in the future.

B006LQQ1GE|4.0|First name Wouter, last name De Backer. But call him Wally, or Gotye - as in Go-tee-yay. Just now hitting the pinnacle of the Target and Best Buy endcaps, the Australia-based Gotye has been selling reasonably well around the world for years now, making diverse discs that remind very much of the tantric spirit of Sting and the new age-y overproduced weirdness of Peter Gabriel. In his native land, the man is the man, his 2006 record, Like Drawing Blood, long-considered to be one of the best pop records the country has ever produced. And now, in the U.S. - and around most of the rest of the civilized world - Gotye is selling big numbers, thanks mostly to his \\"Somebody That I Used to Know\\" single.    But American ears are just now really catching on. Last year Making Mirrors was a phenomenon in Australia and elsewhere, going Platinum on the day it was released in Poland. When I first saw the \\"Somebody\\" video, totally by chance on YouTube one lonely spring night, I was left cold. The track was weird and icy and awkward and the people in the video kinda/sorta put the creeps up my spine. It felt like art made for retail ears, and I don't like art made for retail ears. But then I heard the song - and saw the awkward video - a few more times, here and there around the hood, and started to like it. Mostly, I liked the cutesy and super detailed - but also kinda minimalist spirited - feel of the accompaniment. The big, blaring, super serious and dramatic vocals still put me off at first, but the song got stuck in my head. So much so that I found myself dashing through the Target aisles one night; there to buy some bread and coffee but instead trying to grab the last copy of Making Mirrors before the joint closed shop for the day. I'd been years - or even a decade or more - since I'd bought a compact disc at a big box store from an artist whom I'd never researched. I was curious. And, I suppose, bored.    Since \\"Somebody\\" felt so strange and sexy, I decided to crack open a bottle of wine, slip into my robe and headphones and couch it up, just me and SexyBoyGotye. I recall being almost instantly surprised at the variety of composition I heard over the first few tracks. Sure, the guy has a serious Sting thing going on, but he looks more like a Sebadoh sideman than a sex addict singer, and so the association didn't feel so rape-y. After an awkward and overwhelming first spin I played the disc again. Bottle number two. This time around I found some songs that I really enjoyed, starting with a track called \\"Easy Way Out\\" that sounds more like a Gorillaz banger than anything String ever took to bed. And, of course, \\"Somebody That I Used to Know\\" was good - even if 'ol Wouter was stealing from m'man Elliott Smith.    As I've become increasingly familiar with the record's 12 tracks over the last two weeks I've developed a strange admiration for Gotye. No, I wouldn't say that I actually like most of the songs on Making Mirrors, but I do respect them. Just about any one of the 12 could be played on the radio and stand a chance at having that same weirdo, overly-emo appeal that \\"Somebody\\" has struck the nation with. Every cut has its own thing going on and each song is worth examining, especially if you like weirdo pop music from the last 30ish years. \\"I Feel Better\\" is a Fauxtown gem that I promise you Cee-Lo is playing in his Hummer at this very moment. A future U.S. radio hit, possibly, and one of my favorite tracks on the record. \\"State of the Act\\" is an oddball dub track that wouldn't sound out of place on an album released by a band opening for Animal Collective or The Dirty Projectors.    What I'm saying is this: Gotye is the real deal. I don't really like most of what he does, but I respect it and, as I've become more and more familiar with each song, I've come to kinda/sorta like Making Mirrors as a whole. The man has a lot of big ideas and he knows how to pull them off as a songwriter, multi-instrumentalist, singer and producer. And I got love for that kind of complete artistry. Also, with Coldplay and U2 not doing anything too interesting these days, there's definitely a huge market for a sexy white guy who likes to croon the stadium to sleep for the upper class. Don't be surprised if Gotye ends up being one of the biggest selling artists in the U.S. over the next few years, for better or worse.    Check out more of my film- and music-focused writing at [...]

Now, I know I'm not supposed to notice or worry about that but I do notice because the author makes a point of always reminding me that Reacher buys clothes--occasionally--instead of carrying any with him.  He does occasionally wash some of them but not enough for me to not think about how he must stink much of the time.  That pulls me out of the story, and then I start thinking about how unbelievable some of it is.  That's a four, at best.<br /><br />John Puller is a more complex, believable Reacher, if some readers insist on a comparison.  He has a strong appreciation of family and tries to preserve his own, hard as it is. He appreciates the discipline the Army has taught him, including taking pride in himself, which fosters paying attention to his clothing.  He is a patriot who understands that, while he believes in the U.S. Army and the United States, his eyes are wide open to the weakness, corruption, blind ambition, intransigence and just plain wrong-headedness of individual Army officers and government officials.  That's why he refuses promotions and stays where he can do the job he knows he is best suited for: solving crimes against the citizens of the United States.<br /><br />John Puller is a character I hope Mr. Baldacci will make a prominent part of his literary family.

1849025975|5.0|I haven't read Orwell since I was made to run the gamut of '1984' and 'Animal Farm' in high school, and I'm glad I picked him up again. Orwell manages to make the Spanish civil war (a topic I don't know anything about other than that it was called the Spanish civil war) both fairly comprehensible and at the same time deeply personal. His memories of slogging through trenches covered in filth, scrounging for firewood and in general being bored out of his mind, offers a sobering, sincere look at the life of a solider. He has that droll English wit which allows him to walk a fine line between humorously belittling the messy world of Spanish socialist warfare (and his own mud-soaked involvement in it) and at the same time manages to show why the conflict was so genuinely important as europe started to tilt towards fascism and destruction. This book makes me want to go out and read everything else he ever wrote.

B004IY6API|5.0|I still remember the day I bought Judas Priest's Stained Class album. It was the first time I bought a Priest album and I picked it up along with British Steel (that I was somewhat familiar with) but I was more curious about Stained and that's the one I played first. I wasn't leaving the record store without British Steel for sure, but I still don't know why I picked SC over any of the other Priest albums that were in the store, it's almost as if it talked to me. To this day it remains one of my favorite albums and I consider it my first by Priest and I know there are many special Judas Priest albums but Stained Class is very special to me. Released in 1978, noticeably it was the first album to feature Les Bink on drums with his technical drumming approach that would make him a fan favorite. I feel this is where Priest really found and idealized their Metal sound although there was plenty of hints with their previous two offerings, (and with their next album Killing Machine/Hell Bent for Leather began the leather image of the genre) although most would say that they perfected or defined the sound of Heavy Metal music with British Steel. It's also the first album to feature the Judas Priest logo.<br /><br />Highlights? Many but to me the diamond on this album is \\"Beyond The Realms of Death\\", probably the most complex song in their catalog (and yes, that's a good thing). I don't think Priest reached that level of epic since. The passion in Rob Halford's voice, K.K. Downing and Glen Tipton's mighty celestial guitar work, Les Bink's complementing drumming, the bass... Everything works. A deep, dark song but a beautiful one at that. I cannot, I repeat, cannot stress how amazing this song is. The opener \\"Exciter\\" is as heavy and fast as it gets an opener in 1978 and Rob's voice soars epic heights towards the end, pure Metal done right by the Metal Gods.  \\"White Heat, Red Hot\\" is considerably slower and less aggressive but very effective, great track. \\"Better By You Better Than Me\\" is the most talked about song of the album, a Spooky Tooth cover that was controversial in the suicide of two teenagers. The title track is another highlight although it's one of my least favorites here which doesn't mean much as all the album's tracks are fantastic. \\"Savage\\" was written about the native Indians's point of view in an interesting historical storytelling a la Maiden (Notice how my description makes it sound like \\"Run to the Hills\\", in a sense I suppose...minus the galloping). Halford shines on this track and it's one of the most interesting songs on Stained Class, the one-two punch of \\"Beyond\\" and this left a mark on me. \\"Hero's End\\" is memorable for its breakdown parts (thumping bass is where it's at here) and incredible guitar performances, I like how Rob uses different timbres of voice to do the lead and backing on this particular one it adds such a powerful effect. The live version of \\"Better...\\" is a nice inclusion but nothing special.<br /><br />A previous reviewer said that Stained Class is a link between the Classic Rock of the early 1970's and the early Heavy Metal bands and I think it's a fitting description. Classic Rock in general borrows a lot from the blues sound which is very much present in those band's guitar sound and some bands like Black Sabbath are at the core of it all, a heavy blues band. Judas Priest however, were quite possibly the first band to drop the blues-influences and go for a faster, chugging twin guitars and although their two previous albums, Sad Wings of Destiny (1976) and Sin After Sin (1977) were in heavy territory themselves (their debut Rocka Rolla was blues-ier) Stained Class is a full on Metal attack. Sin After Sin was getting there but even that album had some (excellent) more rock oriented songs and softer like \\"Last Rose of the Summer\\" whereas from the beginning of SC \\"Exciter\\" screams Metal and stays so; it's a heavier type of Rock N' Roll alright. The resulting work is admirable.<br /><br />Perhaps the reason I enjoy Stained Class so much is because it wasn't as popular as albums like British Steel and Screaming For Vengeance and because its songs weren't played that much except \\"Better By You Better Than Me\\" and nearly every song is a gem that stands out. Those well versed into Heavy Metal would do great by picking up Stained Class and fans of Judas Priest would be advised strongly to pick up a copy of this album, it ranks up there with Sin After Sin, Killing Machine, British Steel, Defenders of the Faith and I'll throw in Sad Wings of Destiny as well. With an impressive body of work like Priest's its sometimes easy to overlook every diamond they produced and this is a fantastic album, masterful musicianship, excellent lyrics I'm running out of things to say. A classic Priest AND Metal album. Rating: The highest possible with extra recommendation.aps the reason I enjoy Stained Class so much is because it wasn't as popular as albums like British Steel and Screaming For Vengeance and because its songs weren't played that much except \\"Better By You Better Than Me\\" and nearly every song is a gem that stands out. I am not too old, not too young. This album was originally recorded, mixed and mastered a couple years before I was born and I hate to say it, but those efforts were way more sublime and appropriate for this album, an album of delicate and articulate material.<br /><br />It seems some of the original tracks could not be found for this remix. The most beautiful and endearing vocals ever delivered by Gabriel open the original mixes of this album. But on this version those a capella opening lines are very noticeably different form the original mixes and here on this remix sound more like a patch work of two or three takes. Sometimes the mix feels congested, nasal in the mid-range, sometime vocals are in that mid-range jumble and sometimes they jump into you lap. I dare say I think ONLY the 5.1 mixes were created or even cared about in this very ambitious Genesis re-mix project. Most likely an automated, computer decided upon, stereo mix-down is what we are left with, those of us who still prefer 2 (or 2.1).<br /><br />Nick did a good job on the definitive remasters, especially with The Lamb... I would suggest sticking with those if you want to hear nicely EQed and cleaner versions of the original stereo mixes of any/all Genesis albums. A lot of what was done by the original engineers and producers of those albums really did enhance and solidify Genesis' in-studio presence and overall musical presentation.

B000002AGN|5.0|Dave Brubeck's defining masterpiece, Time Out is one of the most rhythmically innovative albums in jazz history, the first to consciously explore time signatures outside of the standard 4/4 beat or 3/4 waltz time. It was a risky move -- Brubeck's record company wasn't keen on releasing such an arty project, and many critics initially roasted him for tampering with jazz's rhythmic foundation. But for once, public taste was more advanced than that of the critics. Buoyed by a hit single in altoist Paul Desmond's ubiquitous \\"Take Five,\\" Time Out became an unexpectedly huge success, and still ranks as one of the most popular jazz albums ever. That's a testament to Brubeck and Desmond's abilities as composers, because Time Out is full of challenges both subtle and overt -- it's just that they're not jarring. Brubeck's classic \\"Blue Rondo &agrave; la Turk\\" blends jazz with classical form and Turkish folk rhythms, while \\"Take Five,\\" despite its overexposure, really is a masterpiece; listen to how well Desmond's solo phrasing fits the 5/4 meter, and how much Joe Morello's drum solo bends time without getting lost. The other selections are richly melodic as well, and even when the meters are even, the group sets up shifting polyrhythmic counterpoints that nod to African and Eastern musics. Some have come to disdain Time Out as it's become increasingly synonymous with upscale coffeehouse ambience, but as someone once said of Shakespeare, it's really very good in spite of the people who like it. It doesn't just sound sophisticated -- it really is sophisticated music, which lends itself to cerebral appreciation, yet never stops swinging. Countless other musicians built on its pioneering experiments, yet it's amazingly accessible for all its advanced thinking, a rare feat in any art form. This belongs in even the most rudimentary jazz collection.

B0017YZIL4|5.0|As the story goes, Wes would practice his guitar using alternate picking with a guitar pick. However, he started getting complaints about the \\"noise\\" so he began using his thumb so the sound wouldn't bother anyone. After he achieved fame he could play using alternate picking from the callus formed on his thumb. Other jazz musicians named him \\"The Thumb.\\"<br />Additionally he was famous for playing octaves (playing the same note on two different strings at the same time. This, plus the tone of not using a pick, gave him that \\"incredible\\" sound. As a jazz guitarist myself, playing guitar this way is amazing.<br />This is a quartet setting with the great Tommy Flanagan on piano, Percy Heath on bass & Albert Heath on drums. B000002ADT|5.0|Kind of Blue isn't merely an artistic highlight for Miles Davis, it's an album that towers above its peers, a record generally considered as the definitive jazz album, a universally acknowledged standard of excellence. Why does Kind of Blue posses such a mystique? Perhaps because this music never flaunts its genius. It lures listeners in with the slow, luxurious bassline and gentle piano chords of \\"So What.\\" From that moment on, the record never really changes pace -- each tune has a similar relaxed feel, as the music flows easily. Yet Kind of Blue is more than easy listening. It's the pinnacle of modal jazz -- tonality and solos build from the overall key, not chord changes, giving the music a subtly shifting quality. All of this doesn't quite explain why seasoned jazz fans return to this record even after they've memorized every nuance. They return because this is an exceptional band -- Miles, Coltrane, Bill Evans, Cannonball Adderley, Paul Chambers, Jimmy Cobb -- one of the greatest in history, playing at the peak of its power. As Evans said in the original liner notes for the record, the band did not play through any of these pieces prior to recording. Davis laid out the themes before the tape rolled, and then the band improvised. The end results were wondrous and still crackle with vitality. Kind of Blue works on many different levels. It can be played as background music, yet it amply rewards close listening. It is advanced music that is extraordinarily enjoyable. It may be a stretch to say that if you don't like Kind of Blue, you don't like jazz -- but it's hard to imagine it as anything other than a cornerstone of any jazz collection.

B002IFT1ZA|2.0|I tried watching this show because so many people have compared it to Lord of the Rings. However, that comparison is superficial at best. Like LOTR, the producers of Game of Thrones did a good job recreating Medieval style costumes and scenery. That's about the only similarity.<br /><br />In terms of the plot and characters - what really matters - the two couldn't be more different. LOTR has an epic story of war and morality, with fascinating characters and deep character studies. Game of Thrones, by contrast, seems to be populated by a bunch of losers and whores. I've watched 2 episodes - got too bored to finish - and couldn't detect anything resembling an interesting story. There's a few minutes of mysterious blue-eyed monsters killing people in the first episode (what I thought were lousy special effects), but most of the series seems to focus around the drama of the king's family.<br /><br />Even after 2 hours of watching, I barely remembered any of the characters' names, much less cared about them. Except for Sean Bean's character, they all seem equally annoying. The only point I got excited was when I thought the little girl was going to kill the obnoxious blond kid (alas, she didn't). Unlike Tolkien's Hobbits, the series doesn't have a point of view character who pulls the viewer into the world, making it tough to invest in the characters. This is especially problematic when there are so many characters, most of who fail to make much of an impression.<br /><br />On another note, HBO seems obsessed with sex and violence in its shows. As a matter of artistic preference, I don't have a problem with explicit sex or violence scenes, but only if they serve the story or character development. However, in Game of Thrones, the directors certainly weren't subtle in their cinematography. At times, I felt like I was watching hardcore porno. Much of it seemed thrown in there for the sake of titillating audiences when a far more subtle approach would have served just as well.<br /><br />I haven't read the book so I don't know whether the HBO series is doing it justice. However, I'd warn fans of LOTR and other fantasy classics that they'll likely find Game of Thrones disappointing. It's too bad - with the sort of money HBO has, it has the ability to create a genuine fantasy epic TV show, but instead we get Desperate Housewives in a Medieval setting.

He manages to add nuances to the usual clich&eacute;s in the genre to craft a different kind of story where a government assassin finds himself facing ethical and emotional issues that he doesn't expect. While it's hardly a deep-character-development novel, Baldacci succeeds in making his characters interesting and attractive enough to keep you turning the pages.<br /><br />While all thrillers are a bit near the top, if not over it, I found that this one contained elements that made it seem more realistic at times than most of the genre. That was due to careful plotting that I admired while reading the book . . . and even more after finishing it.<br /><br />I also liked the way the plot twists were designed to reveal new clues concerning what is really going on.<br /><br />If you've liked any earlier David Baldacci books, I think you'll like this one.<br /><br />The book begins by establishing the solitary work of Will Robie in getting rid of the bad guys under difficult circumstances. After you are well into that, he comes to a hit that surprises him . . . and leads to some unexpected events. In the course of those events, he meets a runaway girl who attracts his protective instincts. In a role reversal, he ends up trying to look out for her while taking care of his own precarious business. It makes for some amusing scenes and sequences that humanize the thriller and increase its appeal.<br /><br />Enjoy!
